increasingly larger results. Although the relevant prior mass region shrinks rapidly with the particle number, the relative accuracy gets increasingly better. It is well known in statistical physics that the relative uncertainty of observables declines as \(1 / \sqrt{N}\). Moreover, the free energy and hence the log-partition function is extensive, i.e. proportional to \(N\). It is therefore expedient to compute \(\ln (\tilde{Z}) / N\) (Table 31.3). It should be pointed out, however, that this quantity is not constant since the factor \(V_{N}(R)\) is missing for the real partition function \(\ln (Z)\). One can easily check that the relative uncertainty drops indeed as \(1 / \sqrt{N}\). As a matter of fact, with increasing dimension we don’t even need more than a single walker in this unimodal case. The unimportance of the number of walkers \(K\) is surprising as the starting point was the Riemann sum and the quadrature error should depend on \(K\). How come? Skilling proposed a generic model to study the origin of the statistical uncertainty in nested sampling, which we will outline next.

A generic example for challenging problems

In mega-dimensional problems, \(L(X)\) will often have the form of a narrow spike at \(X=0\) rising out of a shallow background. Unfortunately, the spike yields the dominant contribution to the integral. In order to elucidate the behaviour of nested sampling under these circumstances, Skilling proposed [193] the following model: \(L(X)=\mathbb{I}(0 \leq X \leq W)\) with exponentially small width \(W=e^{-\xi}\). Correspondingly, \(Z=e^{-\xi}\). A single nested sampling run generates a sequence \(X_{n}^{*}\) of prior mass values. The contribution to the integral is zero until \(X_{\hat{n}}^{*} \leq W\) at step \(\hat{n}\). In all remaining steps \(L\left(X_{n^{\prime}}^{*}\right)=1\), so the estimated value is

\[ \hat{Z}_{K}=(1-\zeta) \sum_{n=\hat{n}}^{\infty} X_{n}=\zeta^{\hat{n}} \]

Here it is again expedient to use \(\zeta=e^{-1 / K}\). Then, from a single run,

\[ \begin{equation*} \ln \left(\hat{Z}_{K}\right)=\hat{n} \ln (\zeta)=-\frac{\hat{n}}{K} \tag{31.29} \end{equation*} \]

The variability enters through \(\hat{n}\). We will show in the next section that \(\hat{n}\) has a Poisson PDF \(p(\hat{n} \mid \mu)\) with mean \(\langle n\rangle=K \boldsymbol{\xi}:=\mu\) and as such it turns into a Gaussian for large \(\mu\). Consequently, \(\ln (\hat{Z})\) is Gaussian as well. The mean value is

\[ \langle\ln (\hat{Z})\rangle=-\frac{\langle\hat{n}\rangle}{K}=-\xi=\ln (Z) . \]

The result is unbiased and for the variance we obtain

\[ \left\langle(\Delta \ln (\hat{Z}))^{2}\right\rangle=\xi K(\ln (\zeta))^{2}=\frac{\xi}{K} . \]

Hence,

\[ \ln [Z]=-\xi \pm \sqrt{\frac{\xi}{K}} \]

As indicated above, the result is unbiased for all \(K\) and the relative uncertainty of the \(\boldsymbol{\xi}\)-dependent term is \(1 / \sqrt{K \boldsymbol{\xi}}\), again in agreement with the observation in the ideal-gas example. For moderate \(\boldsymbol{\xi}\) the uncertainty can be improved by increasing \(K\), which is not necessary if \(\boldsymbol{\xi}\) itself is already very large. Let us see what happens if we analyse the bare \(\hat{Z}\), given by

\[ \hat{Z}=e^{-\frac{\hat{n}}{K}} . \]

The moments follow from equation (31.29):

\[ \left\langle\hat{Z}^{\nu}\right\rangle=\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} \zeta^{\nu n} p(n \mid \mu)=e^{-\mu} \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} e^{-\frac{\nu n}{K}} \frac{\mu^{n}}{n!}=e^{-\mu\left(1-e^{-\nu / K}\right)} . \]

Expanding the exponent to leading order in \(1 / K\), we obtain

\[ \langle\hat{Z}\rangle=e^{-\xi\left(1-\frac{1}{2 K}\right)}, \quad\left\langle\hat{Z}^{2}\right\rangle=e^{-2 \xi\left(1-\frac{1}{K}\right)} . \]

The mean is off by a factor \(e^{\xi / 2 K}\), which is gigantic, since in real-world applications \(\xi \ggg 1\) and \(K\) can never be chosen big enough to make up for it. For a concrete example, let us suppose that \(\boldsymbol{\xi}=1000\) and \(K=10\), then the logarithmic estimate yields

\[ \ln [Z]=-1000 \pm 10, \]

which is a fairly satisfactory and unbiased result.

The estimate for the bare \(Z\), however, yields

\[ Z=e^{-951.6} \pm e^{-906.3} \]

The mean is orders of magnitude smaller than the standard deviation. Using the prior knowledge ( \(Z>0\) ), we have

\[ Z \in\left[0, e^{-906.3}\right] \]

or rather

\[ \ln (Z) \in[-\infty,-906.3] . \]

This useless result shows that \(\ln (Z)\) is preferred to \(Z\). The impact of \(\ln (Z)\) being Gaussian on the PDF for \(Z\) deserves a closer look. We consider the Gaussian random variable \(x=\ln (Z)\) with

\[ p(x) \propto e^{-\left(x-x_{0}\right)^{2} / 2 \sigma_{x}^{2}} \]

Along with \(p(Z)=p(x) \frac{d x}{d Z}\) we obtain for \(Z\) :

\[ \begin{aligned} & \langle Z\rangle=e^{x_{0}+\sigma_{x}^{2} / 2} \\ & \sigma_{Z}=\sqrt{\left\langle(\Delta Z)^{2}\right\rangle}=e^{x_{0}+\sigma_{x}^{2}}\left(1-e^{-\sigma_{x}^{2}}\right)^{1 / 2} . \end{aligned} \]

Then the relative uncertainty is

\[ \frac{\sigma_{Z}}{\langle Z\rangle}=e^{\sigma_{x}^{2} / 2}\left(1-e^{-\sigma_{x}^{2}}\right)^{1 / 2} \]

Obviously, the relative uncertainty in \(Z\) is only small if \(\sigma_{x}<1\). Then we have

\[ \begin{equation*} \frac{\sigma_{Z}}{\langle Z\rangle}=\sigma_{x} . \tag{31.30} \end{equation*} \]

How long does it take to reach convergence?

An interesting question concerns the number \(\hat{n}\) of steps it takes to enter the spike region, defined by \(X_{\hat{n}}^{*} \leq W=e^{-\xi}\). Based on equation (31.17) [p. 581], we can at least conclude

\[ \hat{n} \approx \frac{|\ln (W)|}{|\ln (\zeta)|}=K \xi . \]

For a detailed analysis and for later discussion it is expedient to introduce \(\Phi:=-\ln \left(X_{1}^{*}\right)\). Along with equation (31.14) [p.580], the corresponding PDF is

\[ \begin{equation*} p(\Phi)=p(\theta) \frac{d \theta}{d \Phi}=K e^{-K \Phi} . \tag{31.31} \end{equation*} \]

Then the sample maximum \(X_{n}^{*}\) of the prior masses in iteration step \(n\), which is according to equation (31.15) [p.581] a product of \(n\) independent factors \(\theta_{\nu}\), translates into a sum \(t_{n}^{*}=\sum_{\nu=1}^{n} \Phi_{\nu}\) of independent and exponentially distributed random variables. This qualifies the values \(t_{n}^{*}\) as Poisson points (see Section 7.8.5 [p. 129]).

In a nested sampling run, let the first step to enter the spike be the \(\hat{n}\) th. Then \(\hat{n}-1\) Poisson points have to precede that, with their values \(t_{\nu}\) not yet having reached the spike edge at \(\boldsymbol{\xi}\). On average, that interval will cover \(\mu=K \boldsymbol{\xi}\) steps. Hence, \(\hat{n}-1\) is Poisson distributed with mean \(\mu\) :

\[ p(\hat{n} \mid \mu)=e^{-\mu} \frac{\mu^{\hat{n}-1}}{(\hat{n}-1)!} . \]

Consequently, the number of steps it takes the walker to enter the relevant prior mass region can be summarized by

\[ \hat{n}-1=K \xi \pm \sqrt{K \xi} \]

This is what we have observed in the ideal-gas case.

31.4 Statistical uncertainty

Now for a realistic problem where we do not know the precise form of \(L\left(X^{*}\right)\) and hence we do not know \(\boldsymbol{\xi}\), how can we estimate the error? The following approach is based on the ideas outlined in [193]. We start out from equation (31.11) [p. 580]:

\[ \begin{equation*} \hat{Z}_{K}=\sum_{n=1}^{n_{\max }} X_{n}^{*} \Delta \lambda_{n}^{*}=\sum_{n=1}^{n_{\max }} e^{-t_{n}^{*}} \Delta \lambda_{n}^{*} \tag{31.32} \end{equation*} \]

We would obtain the correct result within the quadrature error if we knew the correct position \(t_{n}^{*}\) corresponding to the measured \(\lambda_{n}^{*}\). We do not know these values, but we can derive their distribution. According to equation (9.6) [p. 152] \(t_{n}^{*}\), the sum of independent and exponentially distributed random numbers, is Gamma distributed \(p\left(t_{n}^{*}\right)=p_{\Gamma}\left(t_{n}^{*} \mid n, K\right)\), with mean and variance given in equations (7.14c) and (7.14e) [p. 102] \(\left\langle t_{n}^{*}\right\rangle=\frac{n}{K}\) and \(\operatorname{var}\left(t_{n}^{*}\right)=\frac{n}{K^{2}}\). Then a Bayesian analysis, analogous to equation (31.19) [p. 581], yields

\[ \left\langle\ln \left[Z_{K}\right]^{\nu}\right\rangle=\int D T\left(\ln \left[\sum_{n=1}^{n_{\max }} e^{-t_{n}^{*}} \Delta \lambda_{n}^{*}\right]\right)^{\nu} p(T \mid K, \mathcal{I}), \]

with \(\boldsymbol{T}:=\left\{t_{n}^{*}\right\}\). We can easily generate Gamma samples according to \(p(\boldsymbol{T} \mid K, \mathcal{I})\) and evaluate first and second moments of \(\ln (Z)\) numerically. In order to gain insight into the uncertainty of nested sampling, we will also present an analytic approach based on ideas outlined in [193].

We will compute the uncertainty that is introduced if we replace the true \(t_{n}^{*}\) in equation (31.32) by the mean values \(t_{n}=n / K\), resulting in \(\bar{Z}_{K}\). The mean squared difference between the true and the estimated \(\log -Z\) value is

\[ \left\langle\left(\ln \left(\hat{Z}_{K}\right)-\ln \left(\bar{Z}_{K}\right)\right)^{2}\right\rangle=\left\langle\left(\ln \left[\frac{\sum_{n=1}^{n_{\max }} e^{-t_{n}^{*}} \Delta \lambda_{n}^{*}}{\sum_{n=1}^{n_{\max }} e^{-\frac{n}{K}} \Delta \lambda_{n}^{*}}\right]\right)^{2}\right\rangle . \]

Since \(\Delta \lambda_{n}^{*}\) is by construction positive, we can define normalized weights

\[ \rho_{n}:=\frac{e^{-\frac{n}{K}} \Delta \lambda_{n}^{*}}{\sum_{n=1}^{n_{\max }} e^{-\frac{n}{K}} \Delta \lambda_{n}^{*}}, \quad \rho_{n}>0, \quad \sum_{n} \rho_{n}=1 . \]

Then

\[ \begin{equation*} \left\langle\left(\ln \left(\hat{Z}_{K}\right)-\ln \left(\bar{Z}_{K}\right)\right)^{2}\right\rangle=\left\langle\left(\ln \left[\sum_{n=1}^{n_{\max }} \rho_{n} e^{-\Delta t_{n}^{*}}\right]\right)^{2}\right\rangle, \tag{31.33} \end{equation*} \]

with \(\Delta t_{n}^{*}:=t_{n}^{*}-\left\langle t_{n}^{*}\right\rangle\). Now, let \(e^{-\Delta t_{n^{\prime}}^{*}}\) be the greatest contribution to the inner sum of equation (31.33). Then we obtain an upper bound if we replace all other exponentials by the largest one. By virtue of the normalization of the weights, we obtain an upper bound for the variance

\[ \left\langle\left(\ln \left(\hat{Z}_{K}\right)-\ln \left(\bar{Z}_{K}\right)\right)^{2}\right\rangle \leq\left\langle\left(\ln \left[e^{\Delta t_{n^{\prime}}^{*}}\right]\right)^{2}\right\rangle=\left\langle\left(\Delta t_{n^{\prime}}^{*}\right)^{2}\right\rangle=\frac{n^{\prime}}{K^{2}} . \]

Obviously, the upper bound comes from the last term \(n^{\prime}=n_{\text {max }}\), which is defined by the logarithmic compression factor \(n_{\max }=K \boldsymbol{\xi}\).

UPPER BOUND FOR THE VARIANCE OF In [Z]

\[ \begin{equation*} \sqrt{\left\langle\left(\ln \left(\hat{Z}_{K}\right)-\ln \left(\bar{Z}_{K}\right)\right)^{2}\right\rangle} \leq \frac{\sqrt{n_{\max }}}{K}=\sqrt{\frac{\xi}{K}} . \tag{31.34} \end{equation*} \]

Since \(\boldsymbol{\xi}\) is determined by the prior-to-posterior compression, this upper limit demonstrates nested sampling is convergence to \(\ln (Z)\) as \(K \rightarrow \infty\). The last column in Table 31.2 and equation (31.33) contains this upper bound for the ideal-gas example. We see that it is actually very close to the true uncertainty, because firstly we can estimate \(n_{\text {max }}\) very well and secondly the inner sum in equation (31.33) seems to be dominated by one term. We also observe that the upper bound correctly describes the size dependence and that a more accurate determination of the bound is immaterial.

It should be pointed out, however, that equation (31.34) is the distance from the true truncated Riemann sum. It is assumed that the truncation \(n_{\text {max }}\) is under control. Needless to say, this is not always possible. From Figure 31.5 [p. 586] we see that it might be tough to tell from the summands of an individual run whether convergence has been reached, or whether we can use convergence in the likelihood as we have noticed that the run is converged long before the maximum likelihood is reached. For these high-dimensional problems the \(H\) function seems to be the best choice for the scale \(\boldsymbol{\xi}\). More details on the stopping criterion and its influence on the accuracy can be found in [193].

31.5 Concluding remarks

We have seen that nested sampling works very well for high-dimensional applications. It has no problem zooming into isolated peaks of the likelihood function and it provides the relative weights of the peaks correctly. To this end, however, it is decisive that either the walkers can tunnel freely between the peaks or, preferably, there are enough walkers for all separate modes. In the two examples it was simple to generate exact independent samples for the restricted prior. If this is not possible and MCMC has to be invoked for that purpose, then the bias and variance of MCMC have to be taken into account as well. But most of all it will slow down the simulation significantly. We refer the reader to the original publications for the various proposed algorithms [31, 181].

The presented didactic examples did not exploit the full potential of nested sampling. It is straightforward to use nested sampling in order to determine posterior expectation values as well. But more importantly, nested sampling really does open up an important class of multiphase problems, related to phase transitions and mixed phases, that have been inaccessible to standard tempering techniques.

Appendix A

Mathematical compendium

A. 1 Schur complement

In connection with the marginalization rule, the ‘Schur complement’ can be very useful in dealing with matrices in general or covariance matrices in particular, as they occur for example in connection with Gaussian integrals. We start out from a \(2 \times 2\) block representation of the matrix under consideration:

\[ \mathbf{M}=\left(\begin{array}{ll} \mathbf{M}_{11} & \mathbf{M}_{12} \\ \mathbf{M}_{21} & \mathbf{M}_{22} \end{array}\right) \]

The key idea is to transform the matrix into a diagonal block form by upper and lower triangular matrices. In a first step we demand

\[ \begin{aligned} \left(\begin{array}{ll} \mathbf{M}_{11} & \mathbf{M}_{12} \\ \mathbf{M}_{21} & \mathbf{M}_{22} \end{array}\right)\left(\begin{array}{cc} \mathbf{1} & -\mathbf{F}_{12} \\ 0 & \mathbf{1} \end{array}\right) & \stackrel{!}{=}\left(\begin{array}{cc} \mathbf{M}_{11} & 0 \\ \mathbf{M}_{21} & \tilde{\mathbf{M}}_{22} \end{array}\right), \\ \mathbf{F}_{12} & :=\mathbf{M}_{11}^{-1} \mathbf{M}_{12}, \\ \tilde{\mathbf{M}}_{22} & :=\mathbf{M}_{22}-\mathbf{M}_{21} \mathbf{M}_{11}^{-1} \mathbf{M}_{12} . \end{aligned} \]

The quantity \(\tilde{\mathbf{M}}_{22}\) is the so-called Schur complement. In a second step we reach the diagonal form by

\[ \begin{aligned} \left(\begin{array}{cc} \mathbf{1} & 0 \\ -\mathbf{F}_{21} & \mathbf{1} \end{array}\right)\left(\begin{array}{cc} \mathbf{M}_{11} & 0 \\ \mathbf{M}_{21} & \tilde{\mathbf{M}}_{22} \end{array}\right) & \stackrel{!}{=}\left(\begin{array}{cc} \mathbf{M}_{11} & 0 \\ 0 & \tilde{\mathbf{M}}_{22} \end{array}\right), \\ \mathbf{F}_{21} & :=\mathbf{M}_{21} \mathbf{M}_{11}^{-1} . \end{aligned} \]

Hence, in total we have

\[ \left(\begin{array}{cc} \mathbf{1} & 0 \\ -\mathbf{F}_{21} & \mathbf{1} \end{array}\right)\left(\begin{array}{ll} \mathbf{M}_{11} & \mathbf{M}_{12} \\ \mathbf{M}_{21} & \mathbf{M}_{22} \end{array}\right)\left(\begin{array}{cc} \mathbf{1} & -\mathbf{F}_{12} \\ 0 & \mathbf{1} \end{array}\right)=\left(\begin{array}{cc} \mathbf{M}_{11} & 0 \\ 0 & \tilde{\mathbf{M}}_{22} \end{array}\right), \]

or along with

\[ \begin{aligned} & \left(\begin{array}{cc} 1 & \mathbf{F}_{12} \\ 0 & 1 \end{array}\right)^{-1}=\left(\begin{array}{cc} 1 & -\mathbf{F}_{12} \\ 0 & 1 \end{array}\right), \\ & \left(\begin{array}{cc} 1 & 0 \\ \mathbf{F}_{21} & 1 \end{array}\right)^{-1}=\left(\begin{array}{cc} 1 & 0 \\ -\mathbf{F}_{21} & 1 \end{array}\right) \end{aligned} \]

we obtain

Schur complement block diagonalization OF \(2 \times 2\) BLOCK MATRICES

\[ \begin{align*} \left(\begin{array}{ll} \mathbf{M}_{11} & \mathbf{M}_{12} \\ \mathbf{M}_{21} & \mathbf{M}_{22} \end{array}\right) & =\left(\begin{array}{cc} \mathbf{1} & 0 \\ \mathbf{F}_{21} & \mathbf{1} \end{array}\right)\left(\begin{array}{cc} \mathbf{M}_{11} & 0 \\ 0 & \tilde{\mathbf{M}}_{22} \end{array}\right)\left(\begin{array}{cc} \mathbf{1} & \mathbf{F}_{12} \\ 0 & \mathbf{1} \end{array}\right), \tag{A.1a}\\ \mathbf{F}_{12} & =\mathbf{M}_{11}^{-1} \mathbf{M}_{12}, \tag{A.1b}\\ \mathbf{F}_{21} & =\mathbf{M}_{21} \mathbf{M}_{11}^{-1}, \tag{A.1c}\\ \tilde{\mathbf{M}}_{22} & =\mathbf{M}_{22}-\mathbf{M}_{21} \mathbf{M}_{11}^{-1} \mathbf{M}_{12} . \tag{A.1d} \end{align*} \]

So we immediately get

\[ \begin{equation*} \operatorname{det}(\mathbf{M})=\operatorname{det}\left(\mathbf{M}_{11}\right) \operatorname{det}\left(\tilde{\mathbf{M}}_{22}\right) \tag{A.2} \end{equation*} \]

and

\[ \mathbf{M}^{-1}=\left(\begin{array}{cc} \mathbf{1} & -\mathbf{F}_{12} \tag{A.3}\\ 0 & \mathbf{1} \end{array}\right)\left(\begin{array}{cc} \mathbf{M}_{11}^{-1} & 0 \\ 0 & \tilde{\mathbf{M}}_{22}^{-1} \end{array}\right)\left(\begin{array}{cc} \mathbf{1} & 0 \\ -\mathbf{F}_{21} & \mathbf{1} \end{array}\right) . \]

For some applications it is useful to determine the inverse matrix explicitly:

\[ \begin{aligned} \mathbf{M}^{-1} & =\left(\begin{array}{cc} \mathbf{1} & -\mathbf{F}_{12} \\ 0 & \mathbf{1} \end{array}\right)\left(\begin{array}{cc} \mathbf{M}_{11}^{-1} & 0 \\ -\tilde{\mathbf{M}}_{22}^{-1} \mathbf{F}_{21} & \tilde{\mathbf{M}}_{22}^{-1} \end{array}\right) \\ & =\left(\begin{array}{cc} \mathbf{M}_{11}^{-1}+\mathbf{F}_{12} \tilde{\mathbf{M}}_{22}^{-1} \mathbf{F}_{21} & -\mathbf{F}_{12} \tilde{\mathbf{M}}_{22}^{-1} \\ -\tilde{\mathbf{M}}_{22}^{-1} \mathbf{F}_{21} & \tilde{\mathbf{M}}_{22}^{-1} \end{array}\right) . \end{aligned} \]

Inverse of a \(2 \times 2\) block matrix

\[ \begin{align*} \left(\begin{array}{ll} \mathbf{M}_{11} & \mathbf{M}_{12} \\ \mathbf{M}_{21} & \mathbf{M}_{22} \end{array}\right)^{-1} & =\left(\begin{array}{ll} B_{11} & B_{12} \\ B_{12}^{T} & B_{22} \end{array}\right), \tag{A.4a}\\ B_{11} & :=\left[\mathbf{M}_{11}^{-1}+\mathbf{M}_{11}^{-1} \mathbf{M}_{12} \tilde{\mathbf{M}}_{22}^{-1} \mathbf{M}_{21} \mathbf{M}_{11}^{-1}\right], \tag{A.4b}\\ B_{12} & :=-\left[\mathbf{M}_{11}^{-1} \mathbf{M}_{12} \tilde{\mathbf{M}}_{22}^{-1}\right], \tag{A.4c}\\ B_{22} & :=\left[\mathbf{M}_{22}-\mathbf{M}_{21} \mathbf{M}_{11}^{-1} \mathbf{M}_{12}\right]^{-1} . \tag{A.4d} \end{align*} \]

A.1.1 Application of the Schur complement

Let us assume we are dealing with a multivariate Gaussian likelihood

\[ \begin{aligned} p\left(x \mid x_{0}, \mathbf{C}, \mathcal{I}\right) & =\frac{1}{(2 \pi)^{\frac{N}{2}}}|\mathbf{C}|^{-\frac{1}{2}} \exp \left\{-\frac{1}{2} \Delta x^{T} \mathbf{C}^{-1} \Delta x\right\} \\ \Delta x & :=x-x_{0} \end{aligned} \]

and we are interested in the marginal distributions of some elements \(x_{i}\) of \(x\). Without loss of generality, we consider the marginal distribution of the first \(l\) elements of \(\boldsymbol{x}\). To this end, we introduce the following abbreviations:

\[ x=\binom{x_{1}}{x_{2}}, x_{0}:=\binom{x_{1,0}}{x_{2,0}} \text { and } \Delta x:=\binom{\Delta x_{1}}{\Delta x_{2}} \text {, with } \Delta x_{\alpha}=x_{\alpha}-x_{\alpha, 0} \]

The dimensions of the subvectors \(x_{1}\) and \(x_{2}\) are \(l\) and \(m\), respectively. Then the sought-for marginal PDF is

\[ \begin{equation*} p\left(x_{2} \mid x_{0}, \mathbf{C}, \mathcal{I}\right) \propto \int d x_{2}^{m} \exp \left\{-\frac{1}{2} \Delta x^{T} \mathbf{C}^{-1} \Delta x\right\} \tag{A.5} \end{equation*} \]

In order to avoid unnecessary computations, it is expedient to suppress all prefactors and determine the normalization at the end. We proceed by first splitting the real symmetric matrix \(\mathbf{C}\) into a \(2 \times 2\) block structure and, invoking equation (A.2), we obtain

\[ \begin{aligned} \Delta \boldsymbol{x}^{T} \mathbf{C}^{-1} \Delta \boldsymbol{x} & =\binom{\Delta \boldsymbol{x}_{1}}{\Delta \boldsymbol{x}_{2}}^{T}\left(\begin{array}{cc} \mathbf{C}_{11} & \mathbf{C}_{12} \\ \mathbf{C}_{21} & \mathbf{C}_{22} \end{array}\right)^{-1}\binom{\Delta \boldsymbol{x}_{1}}{\Delta \boldsymbol{x}_{2}} \\ & =\binom{\Delta \boldsymbol{x}_{1}}{\Delta \boldsymbol{x}_{2}}^{T}\left(\begin{array}{cc} \mathbf{1} & -\mathbf{F}_{12} \\ & \mathbf{1} \end{array}\right)\left(\begin{array}{cc} \mathbf{C}_{11}^{-1} & 0 \\ & \tilde{\mathbf{C}}_{22}^{-1} \end{array}\right)\left(\begin{array}{cc} \mathbf{1} & 0 \\ -\mathbf{F}_{21} & \mathbf{1} \end{array}\right)\binom{\Delta \boldsymbol{x}_{1}}{\Delta \boldsymbol{x}_{2}} \\ & =\binom{\Delta \boldsymbol{x}_{1}}{\Delta \boldsymbol{x}_{2}-\mathbf{F}_{21} \Delta \boldsymbol{x}_{1}}^{T}\left(\begin{array}{cc} \mathbf{M}_{11} & 0 \\ & \tilde{\mathbf{M}}_{22} \end{array}\right)\binom{\Delta \boldsymbol{x}_{1}}{\Delta \boldsymbol{x}_{2}-\mathbf{F}_{21} \Delta \boldsymbol{x}_{1}} . \end{aligned} \]

We have used the fact that \(\mathbf{F}_{21}=\mathbf{F}_{12}^{T}\) for real symmetric \(\mathbf{C}\). For the following calculation we don’t need the definition of \(\mathbf{F}_{12}\) and \(\tilde{\mathbf{C}}_{22}\). Next we substitute \(\boldsymbol{x}_{2} \rightarrow \boldsymbol{x}_{2}^{\prime}:=\Delta \boldsymbol{x}_{2}- \mathbf{F}_{21} \Delta \boldsymbol{x}_{1}\) to obtain

\[ \begin{aligned} \Delta x^{T} \mathbf{C}^{-1} \Delta x & =\binom{\Delta x_{1}}{x_{2}^{\prime}}^{T}\left(\begin{array}{cc} \mathbf{C}_{11}^{-1} & 0 \\ & \tilde{\mathbf{C}}_{22}^{-1} \end{array}\right)\binom{\Delta x_{1}}{x_{2}^{\prime}} \\ & =\left(\Delta x_{1}\right)^{T} \mathbf{C}_{11}^{-1} \Delta x_{1}+\left(x_{2}^{\prime}\right)^{T} \tilde{\mathbf{C}}_{22}^{-1} x_{2}^{\prime} \end{aligned} \]

Now the integral in equation (A.5) over \(x_{2}^{\prime}\) is independent of \(\Delta x_{1}\) and is therefore merely part of the normalization. The result reads

\[ \begin{equation*} p\left(x_{2} \mid x_{0}, \mathbf{C}, \mathcal{I}\right) \propto \exp \left\{-\frac{1}{2}\left(\Delta x_{1}\right)^{T} \mathbf{C}_{11}^{-1} \Delta x_{1}\right\} \tag{A.6} \end{equation*} \]

Marginal Gaussian PDF

\[ \begin{equation*} p\left(\boldsymbol{x}_{1} \mid \boldsymbol{x}_{0}, \mathbf{C}, \mathcal{I}\right)=\frac{\left|\mathbf{C}_{11}\right|^{-\frac{1}{2}}}{(2 \pi)^{\frac{l}{2}}} \exp \left\{-\frac{1}{2}\left(\Delta \boldsymbol{x}_{1}\right)^{T} \mathbf{C}_{11}^{-1} \Delta \boldsymbol{x}_{1}\right\} . \tag{A.7} \end{equation*} \]

It is noteworthy that \(\mathbf{C}_{11}^{-1}\) is the inverse of the first block \(\mathbf{C}_{11}\) of \(\mathbf{C}\) and not the first block of the inverse of \(\mathbf{C}^{-1}\). In particular, if \(\boldsymbol{x}_{1}\) has size \(l=1\) and we want the marginal PDF for a single variable, \(x_{i}\) say, then the result is

\[ \begin{equation*} p\left(x_{i} \mid x_{0}, \mathbf{C}, \mathcal{I}\right)=\frac{1}{\sqrt{2 \pi C_{i i}}} e^{-\frac{\left(\Delta x_{i}\right)^{2}}{2 C_{i i}}} \tag{A.8} \end{equation*} \]

A. 2 Saddle-point approximation

One of the ubiquitous mathematical tasks in Bayesian probability theory is the evaluation of multidimensional integrals of the form

\[ I_{f}:=\int_{V} f(x) p(x) d^{N} x \]

where \(p(x)\) stands for a PDF and is innately positive, and as such can be expressed as \(p(x)=\exp [\Phi(x)]\). Usually \(f(x)\) is slowly varying compared with \(p(x)\). If \(f(x)\) is also positive, it can be incorporated into \(\Phi(\boldsymbol{x})\). An approximation often used in statistical physics for such integrals is the ‘saddle-point approximation’, which consists of two steps. The first is the Taylor expansion of \(\Phi(\boldsymbol{x})\) about its maximum up to second order. The position \(x^{*}\) of the maximum is, as usual, determined via

\[ \left.\nabla_{x} \Phi(x)\right|_{x=x^{*}}=0 \]

and \(\Phi(\boldsymbol{x})\) is then replaced by

\[ \begin{aligned} \Phi(x) & \approx \Phi\left(x^{*}\right)+\frac{1}{2}\left(x-x^{*}\right)^{T} \mathbf{H}\left(x-x^{*}\right), \\ \mathbf{H} & :=\left.\nabla \nabla^{T} \Phi(x)\right|_{x=x^{*}} . \end{aligned} \]

The matrix \(\mathbf{H}\) is the Hesse matrix. The second step of the saddle-point approximation is to replace the volume of integration \(V\) by \(\mathbb{R}^{N}\). Then the remaining integral is a standard Gaussian integral. We will exemplify the saddle-point approximation guided by Stirling’s formula for the Gamma function.

A.2.1 Saddle-point approximation for \(\Gamma(x)\)

We start out from the definition of the Gamma function

\[ \Gamma(z)=\int_{0}^{\infty} x^{z-1} e^{-x} d x=\int_{0}^{\infty} e^{-x+(z-1) \ln (x)} d x \]

Next we determine the maximum of the argument

\[ \Phi(x)=-x+(z-1) \ln (x) \]

of the exponential

\[ \begin{aligned} \frac{d \Phi(x)}{d x} & =-1+\frac{z-1}{x}=0 \\ \Rightarrow \quad x^{*} & =z-1 \\ \Phi\left(x^{*}\right) & =(z-1) \ln (z-1)-(z-1) . \end{aligned} \]

The second derivative yields

\[ H=\left.\frac{d^{2} \Phi(x)}{d x^{2}}\right|_{x^{*}}=-\frac{z-1}{\left(x^{*}\right)^{2}}=-\frac{1}{z-1}, \]

and according to the first step of the saddle-point approximation the exponential is replaced by

\[ e^{\Phi} \approx e^{\Phi\left(x^{*}\right)} e^{-\frac{1}{2(z-1)}\left(x-x^{*}\right)^{2}} \]

and the integral along with the second step of the saddle-point approximation yields

\[ \begin{aligned} \Gamma(z) & \approx e^{\Phi\left(x^{*}\right)} \cdot \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} e^{-\frac{1}{2(z-1)}\left(x-x^{*}\right)^{2}} d x \\ & \approx(z-1)^{(z-1)} e^{-(z-1)} \cdot \sqrt{2 \pi(z-1)} \\ & \approx(z-1)^{(z-1 / 2)} e^{-(z-1)} \sqrt{2 \pi} \end{aligned} \]

For integer values \(z=N+1\) we obtain Stirling’s formula for the factorial

\[ N!=\Gamma(N+1) \approx N^{(N+1 / 2)} e^{-N} \sqrt{2 \pi} . \]

A. 3 Useful expressions for \(\operatorname{det}(\mathbf{A})\)

A.3.1 \(\ln (\operatorname{det}(\mathbf{A}))=\operatorname{tr}(\ln (\mathbf{A}))\)

We first prove the relation

DETERMINANT OF SQUARE MATRICES

\[ \begin{equation*} \ln (\operatorname{det}(\mathbf{A}))=\operatorname{tr}(\ln (\mathbf{A})) \tag{A.9} \end{equation*} \]

for a \(N \times N\) square matrix \(\mathbf{A}\). This representation can be very useful for the numerical evaluation of determinants of huge matrices or for the derivative of determinants w.r.t. some parameters.

For the proof we invoke the Jordan normal form of the square matrix \(\mathbf{A}\) :

\[ \begin{equation*} \mathbf{A}=\mathbf{Q} \mathbf{J} \mathbf{Q}^{-1}, \tag{A.10} \end{equation*} \]

where the Jordan normal form \(\mathbf{J}\) is block diagonal and consists of so-called Jordan matrices, i.e. \(\mathbf{J}=\operatorname{diag}\left(\left\{\mathbf{J}_{v}\right\}\right)\), for \(v=1, \ldots, L\). The Jordan blocks \(\mathbf{J}_{v}\) have the special form

\[ \begin{align*} \mathbf{J}_{\nu} & =\lambda_{\nu} \mathbf{1}+\Delta_{\nu}, \tag{A.11}\\ \Delta_{\nu} & :=\left(\begin{array}{llll} 0 & 1 & & \\ & 0 & 1 & \\ & & \ddots & \ddots \end{array}\right) . \end{align*} \]

The size of the Jordan block \(\mathbf{J}_{\nu}\) is equal to the degeneracy \(n_{\nu}\) of the eigenvalue \(\lambda_{\nu}\). Consequently, \(\mathbf{J}\) can be decomposed as

\[ \begin{equation*} \mathbf{J}=\mathbf{D}+\mathbf{\Delta} \tag{A.12} \end{equation*} \]

into the diagonal part \(\mathbf{D}=\operatorname{diag}\left(\left\{\lambda_{i}\right\}\right)\) containing the eigenvalues \(\lambda_{i}\) and an off-diagonal part \(\boldsymbol{\Delta}\) which has only nonzero entries on the superdiagonal. The entries on the superdiagonal are either zero or one. Hence the derivative w.r.t. some parameter affects only the diagonal and hence the eigenvalues

\[ \begin{equation*} \frac{d}{d t} \mathbf{J}(t)=\frac{d}{d t} \mathbf{D}(t) . \tag{A.13} \end{equation*} \]

Based on the property \(\operatorname{tr}\left(\boldsymbol{\Delta}^{m}\right)=0\), for \(m \in \mathbb{N}\), straightforward Taylor expansion yields ( \(\lambda_{\nu} \neq 0\) since \(\operatorname{det}(\mathbf{A}) \neq 0\) )

\[ \operatorname{tr}\left[\ln \left(\mathbf{J}_{\nu}\right)\right]=\operatorname{tr}\left[\ln \left(\lambda_{\nu} \mathbf{1}\right)\right]=n_{\nu} \ln \left(\lambda_{\nu}\right) . \]

Consequently,

\[ \begin{aligned} \operatorname{tr}[\ln (\mathbf{A})] & =\operatorname{tr}\left[\ln \left(\mathbf{Q} \mathbf{J} \mathbf{Q}^{-1}\right)\right]=\operatorname{tr}\left[\mathbf{Q} \ln (\mathbf{J}) \mathbf{Q}^{-1}\right]=\operatorname{tr}[\ln (\mathbf{J})] \\ & =\sum_{\nu=1}^{L} n_{\nu} \ln \left(\lambda_{\nu}\right)=\sum_{i=1}^{N} \ln \left(\lambda_{i}\right) \end{aligned} \]

In the last sum degenerate eigenvalues occur multiply according to their degeneracy. Moreover,

\[ \begin{aligned} \sum_{i=1}^{N} \ln \left(\lambda_{i}\right) & =\ln \left[\prod\left(\lambda_{i}\right)\right]=\ln [\operatorname{det}(\mathbf{J})]=\ln \left[\operatorname{det}\left(\mathbf{Q}^{-1} \mathbf{A} \mathbf{Q}\right)\right] \\ & =\ln \left[\operatorname{det}\left(\mathbf{Q}^{-1}\right) \operatorname{det}(\mathbf{A}) \operatorname{det}(\mathbf{Q})\right]=\ln [\operatorname{det}(\mathbf{A})] \end{aligned} \]

This finishes the proof for equation (A.9).

A.3.2 Derivative of determinants w.r.t. parameters

In many cases we need the derivative of some function that contains the determinant of a matrix \(\mathbf{A}(t)\). We concentrate therefore on the evaluation of

\[ \frac{d}{d t} \operatorname{det}[\mathbf{A}(t)] . \]

Based on an alternative form of equation (A.9),

\[ \operatorname{det}(\mathbf{A}(t))=\exp \{\operatorname{tr}[\ln (\mathbf{A}(t))]\}, \]

we obtain

\[ \begin{align*} \frac{d}{d t} \operatorname{det}(\mathbf{A}(t)) & =\exp \{\operatorname{tr}[\ln (\mathbf{A}(t))]\} \frac{d}{d t} \operatorname{tr}[\ln (\mathbf{A}(t))] \\ & =\operatorname{det}(\mathbf{A}(t)) \operatorname{tr}\left[\frac{d}{d t} \ln (\mathbf{A}(t))\right] \\ & =\operatorname{det}(\mathbf{A}(t)) \operatorname{tr}\left[[\mathbf{A}(t)]^{-1} \frac{d}{d t} \mathbf{A}(t)\right] \tag{A.14} \end{align*} \]

Next, we will prove that

\[ \operatorname{tr}\left[[\mathbf{A}(t)]^{-1} \frac{d}{d t} \mathbf{A}(t)\right]=\operatorname{tr}\left[[\mathbf{D}(t)]^{-1} \frac{d}{d t} \mathbf{D}(t)\right] . \]

Based on the similarity transformation equation (A.10), we get

\[ \operatorname{tr}\left[[\mathbf{A}(t)]^{-1} \frac{d}{d t} \mathbf{A}(t)\right], \]

for which the two factors can be transformed according to equation (A.10):

\[ [\mathbf{A}(t)]^{-1}=\mathbf{Q} \mathbf{J}^{-1} \mathbf{Q}^{-1} \]

and

\[ \frac{d}{d t}[\mathbf{A}(t)]=\left(\frac{d}{d t} \mathbf{Q}\right) \mathbf{J} \mathbf{Q}^{-1}+\mathbf{Q}\left(\frac{d}{d t} \mathbf{J}\right) \mathbf{Q}^{-1}+\mathbf{Q J}\left(\frac{d}{d t} \mathbf{Q}^{-1}\right) \]

Multiplying the two factors and using the cyclic invariance of the trace yields

\[ \operatorname{tr}\left[[\mathbf{A}(t)]^{-1} \frac{d}{d t} \mathbf{A}(t)\right]=\underbrace{\operatorname{tr}\left[\mathbf{Q}^{-1}\left(\frac{d}{d t} \mathbf{Q}\right)\right]+\operatorname{tr}\left[\left(\frac{d}{d t} \mathbf{Q}^{-1}\right) \mathbf{Q}\right]}_{=\frac{d}{d t} \operatorname{tr}\left[\mathbf{Q}^{-1} \mathbf{Q}\right]=0}+\operatorname{tr}\left[\mathbf{J}^{-1}\left(\frac{d}{d t} \mathbf{J}\right)\right] . \]

Now, according to equations (A.13) and (A.12), we have

\[ \operatorname{tr}\left[\mathbf{J}^{-1} \frac{d}{d t} \mathbf{J}\right]=\operatorname{tr}\left[\mathbf{J}^{-1} \frac{d}{d t} \mathbf{D}\right]=\sum_{i=1}^{N}\left(\mathbf{J}^{-1}\right)_{i i} \frac{d}{d t} \lambda_{i} . \]

Since \(\Delta^{m}\) has no diagonal entries for \(m>0\), only \(\mathbf{D}\) contributes to the diagonal of \(\mathbf{J}^{-1}\) and we obtain

\[ \operatorname{tr}\left[[\mathbf{A}(t)]^{-1} \frac{d}{d t} \mathbf{A}(t)\right]=\operatorname{tr}\left[\mathbf{D}^{-1} \frac{d}{d t} \mathbf{D}\right] . \]

Eventually, we continue with equation (A.14) and find

\[ \begin{align*} \frac{d}{d t} \operatorname{det}(\mathbf{A}(t)) & =\operatorname{det}(\mathbf{A}(t)) \operatorname{tr}\left[[\mathbf{D}(t)]^{-1} \frac{d}{d t} \mathbf{D}(t)\right] \tag{A.15}\\ & =\operatorname{det}(\mathbf{A}(t)) \sum_{i=1}^{N} \frac{d}{d t} \ln \left(\lambda_{i}(t)\right) \tag{A.16} \end{align*} \]

which is a generalization of the Hellmann-Feynman theorem that can be useful both for numerical and analytical calculations.

A. 4 Multivariate Gauss integrals

We will often encounter multivariate Gauss integrals, either because the PDF under consideration is Gaussian or because the saddle-point approximation is invoked. The most general form of a Gaussian integral is

\[ \begin{equation*} \boldsymbol{I}=\int \exp \left\{-\frac{1}{2} \boldsymbol{x}^{T} \mathbf{A} \boldsymbol{x}+\boldsymbol{b}^{T} \boldsymbol{x}\right\} d^{N} x \tag{A.17} \end{equation*} \]

Without loss of generality we assume that \(\mathbf{A}\) is real symmetric, otherwise we use the fact that ( \(\boldsymbol{x}^{T} \mathbf{A x}\) ) is real and therefore ( \(\left.\boldsymbol{x}^{T} \mathbf{A x}\right)^{*}=\left(\boldsymbol{x}^{T} \mathbf{A}^{T} \boldsymbol{x}\right)\) holds. Hence

\[ \left(\boldsymbol{x}^{T} \mathbf{A} \boldsymbol{x}\right)=\frac{1}{2}\left(\left(\boldsymbol{x}^{T} \mathbf{A} \boldsymbol{x}\right)+\left(\boldsymbol{x}^{T} \mathbf{A}^{T} \boldsymbol{x}\right)\right)=\boldsymbol{x}^{T} \underbrace{\frac{\mathbf{A}+\mathbf{A}^{T}}{2}}_{\rightarrow \mathbf{A}} \boldsymbol{x} \text {. } \]

The argument of the Gaussian

\[ \Phi(x)=-\frac{1}{2} x^{T} \mathbf{A} x+b^{T} x \]

has its maximum at

\[ x^{*}=\mathbf{A}^{-1} b, \]

with

\[ \Phi\left(\boldsymbol{x}^{*}\right)=\frac{1}{2} \boldsymbol{b}^{T} \mathbf{A}^{-1} \boldsymbol{b} \]

Hence

\[ \Phi(x)=\Phi\left(x^{*}\right)-\frac{1}{2}\left(x-x^{*}\right)^{T} \mathbf{A}\left(x-x^{*}\right) \]

For the integration over \(\boldsymbol{x}\) we introduce the substitution \(\boldsymbol{x} \rightarrow \boldsymbol{z}=\boldsymbol{x}-\boldsymbol{x}^{*}\) and get

\[ I=\exp \left\{\frac{1}{2} \boldsymbol{b}^{T} \mathbf{A}^{-1} \boldsymbol{b}\right\} \int \exp \left\{-\frac{1}{2} \boldsymbol{z}^{T} \mathbf{A} \boldsymbol{z}\right\} d^{N} z \]

Since \(\mathbf{A}\) is real symmetric, it can be cast into the form

\[ \mathbf{A}=\mathbf{U D U}^{T} \]

with \(\mathbf{U}\) being a unitary matrix, which contains the eigenvectors of \(\mathbf{A}\) in its columns, and \(\mathbf{D}=\operatorname{diag}\left\{\lambda_{i}\right\}\) the diagonal matrix of the eigenvalues of \(\mathbf{A}\). The next step is a further substitution \(\mathbf{U}^{T} \boldsymbol{z}:=\boldsymbol{y}\). Since \(\mathbf{U}\) is unitary we find

\[ I=e^{\frac{1}{2} \boldsymbol{b}^{T} \mathbf{A}^{-1} \boldsymbol{b}} \prod_{i=1}^{N}\left\{\int e^{-\frac{1}{2} \lambda_{i} y_{i}^{2}} d y_{i}\right\} \]

Obviously, all eigenvalues have to be greater than zero, otherwise the corresponding integral diverges. In other words, \(\mathbf{A}\) has to be strictly positive. If this is the case, we obtain

\[ \begin{aligned} I & =\exp \left\{\frac{1}{2} \boldsymbol{b}^{T} \mathbf{A}^{-1} \boldsymbol{b}\right\}(2 \pi)^{N / 2} \prod_{i=1}^{N} \lambda_{i}^{-1 / 2} \\ & =(2 \pi)^{N / 2}|\mathbf{A}|^{-1 / 2} \exp \left\{\frac{1}{2} \boldsymbol{b}^{T} \mathbf{A}^{-1} \boldsymbol{b}\right\} \end{aligned} \]

The final result reads

Gauss integrals

\[ \begin{equation*} \int \exp \left\{-\frac{1}{2} \boldsymbol{x}^{T} \mathbf{A} \boldsymbol{x}+\boldsymbol{b}^{T} \boldsymbol{x}\right\} d^{N} x=(2 \pi)^{\frac{N}{2}}|\mathbf{A}|^{-\frac{1}{2}} \quad \exp \left\{\frac{1}{2} \boldsymbol{b}^{T} \mathbf{A}^{-1} \boldsymbol{b}\right\} . \tag{A.18} \end{equation*} \]

If \(\mathbf{A} \neq \mathbf{A}^{T}\) replace it by \(\left(\mathbf{A}+\mathbf{A}^{T}\right) / 2\).

A. 5 Sums containing binomial coefficients

Here we list some useful relations containing (negative) binomial coefficients, that will be used in this book, along with the proofs. If not stated otherwise, we use the convention \(n, k, v, \mu \in \mathbb{N}_{0}\) and \(a, b, r \in \mathbb{R}\). To begin with we repeat the definition of the binomial coefficient:

\[ \binom{r}{n}:= \begin{cases}\frac{r_{(n)}}{n!} & \text { for } n \geq 0 \tag{A.19}\\ 0 & \text { otherwise. }\end{cases} \]

We begin with the relation

\[ \begin{equation*} \binom{r}{n-1}+\binom{r}{n}=\binom{r+1}{n} . \tag{A.20} \end{equation*} \]

Proof:

\[ \begin{aligned} \binom{r}{n-1}+\binom{r}{n} & =\frac{r_{(n-1)}}{(n-1)!}+\frac{r_{(n)}}{n!} \\ & =\frac{n r_{(n-1)}+(r-(n-1)) r_{(n-1)}}{n!} \\ & =\frac{r_{(n-1)}(r+1)}{n!} \\ & =\frac{(r+1)_{(n)}}{n!} . \end{aligned} \] \(\square\)

\[ \begin{equation*} \sum_{\nu=0}^{k}\binom{n}{\nu}\binom{n-\nu}{k-\nu} x^{\nu}=\binom{n}{k}(1+x)^{k} . \tag{A.21} \end{equation*} \]

Proof:

\[ \begin{aligned} \sum_{\nu=0}^{k}\binom{n}{\nu}\binom{n-\nu}{k-\nu} x^{\nu} & =\sum_{\nu=0}^{k} \frac{n!}{(n-\nu)!\nu!} \frac{(n-\nu)!}{(n-k)!(k-\nu)!} x^{\nu} \\ & =\binom{n}{k} \sum_{\nu=0}^{k}\binom{k}{\nu} x^{\nu} \\ & =\binom{n}{k}(1+x)^{k} . \end{aligned} \] \(\square\)

\[ \begin{equation*} \binom{-r}{n}=(-1)^{n}\binom{n+r-1}{n} . \tag{A.22} \end{equation*} \]

Proof:

\[ \begin{aligned} \binom{-r}{n} & =\frac{(-r)(-r-1) \cdots(-r-(n-1))}{n!} \\ & =(-1)^{n} \frac{(r+n-1)(r+n-2) \cdots r}{n!} \\ & =(-1)^{n}\binom{n+r-1}{n} \end{aligned} \] \(\square\)

\[ \begin{equation*} \binom{-\frac{1}{2}}{n}=\left(-\frac{1}{4}\right)^{n}\binom{2 n}{n} . \tag{A.23} \end{equation*} \]

Proof:

\[ \begin{aligned} \binom{-\frac{1}{2}}{n^{2}} & =\frac{\left(-\frac{1}{2}\right)\left(-\frac{1}{2}-1\right)\left(-\frac{1}{2}-2\right) \cdots\left(-\frac{1}{2}-(n-1)\right)}{n!} \\ & =\left(-\frac{1}{2}\right)^{n} \frac{(1)(1+2)(1+4) \cdots(1+2(n-1))}{n!} \\ & =\left(-\frac{1}{2}\right)^{n} \frac{(2 n-1)!!}{n!} \\ & =\left(-\frac{1}{2}\right)^{n} \frac{(2 n)!}{2 \cdot 4 \cdots(2 n) n!} \\ & =\left(-\frac{1}{2}\right)^{n} \frac{(2 n)!}{2^{n} n!n!} \\ & =\left(-\frac{1}{4}\right)^{n}\binom{2 n}{n} . \end{aligned} \]

\[ \begin{align*} & \binom{\frac{1}{2}}{n}=-\frac{1}{2 n-1}\left(-\frac{1}{4}\right)^{n}\binom{2 n}{n}, \tag{A.24a}\\ & \binom{\frac{1}{2}}{n}=-\frac{1}{2 n-1}\binom{-\frac{1}{2}}{n} . \tag{A.24b} \end{align*} \]

Proof: For \(n=1\) we find directly that both equations are correct. For \(n>1\) we obtain

\[ \begin{aligned} \binom{\frac{1}{2}}{n} & =\frac{\left(\frac{1}{2}\right)\left(\frac{1}{2}-1\right)\left(\frac{1}{2}-2\right) \cdots\left(\frac{1}{2}-(n-1)\right)}{n!} \\ & =\frac{\left(\frac{1}{2}\right)\left(-\frac{1}{2}\right)\left(-\frac{1}{2}-1\right) \cdots\left(-\frac{1}{2}-(n-2)\right)}{n!} \\ & =-\left(-\frac{1}{2}\right)^{n} \frac{(1)(1)(1+2)(1+4) \cdots(1+2(n-2))}{n!} \\ & =-\left(-\frac{1}{2}\right)^{n} \frac{(2 n-3)!!}{n!} \\ & =-\left(-\frac{1}{2}\right)^{n} \frac{(2 n-2)!}{2 \cdot 4 \cdots(2(n-1)) n!} \end{aligned} \]

\[ \begin{aligned} & =-\left(-\frac{1}{2}\right)^{n} \frac{(2 n-2)!(2 n-1)(2 n)}{2^{n-1}(n-1)!n!(2 n-1)(2 n)} \\ & =-\left(-\frac{1}{4}\right)^{n} \frac{(2 n)!}{n!n!(2 n-1)} \\ & =-\frac{1}{2 n-1}\left(-\frac{1}{4}\right)^{n}\binom{2 n}{n} \end{aligned} \]

Equation (A.24b) follows from equations (A.24a) and (A.23).

\[ \begin{equation*} \sum_{\nu=0}^{n}\binom{r}{\nu}(-1)^{\nu}=(-1)^{n}\binom{r-1}{n} \tag{A.25} \end{equation*} \]

Proof: We prove the relation by induction on \(n\). The relation is fulfilled trivially for \(n=0\). We assume it is correct for all \(n\) with \(n \leq m\). Then

\[ \begin{aligned} \sum_{\nu=0}^{m+1}\binom{r}{\nu}(-1)^{\nu} & =\sum_{\nu=0}^{m}\binom{r}{\nu}(-1)^{\nu}+\binom{r}{m+1}(-1)^{m+1} \\ & =(-1)^{m}\binom{r-1}{m}-(-1)^{m}\binom{r}{m+1} \end{aligned} \]

According to equation (A.20) this yields

\[ \begin{aligned} & =(-1)^{m}\left[\binom{r-1}{m}-\binom{r-1}{m}-\binom{r-1}{m+1}\right] \\ & =(-1)^{m+1}\binom{r-1}{m+1} . \end{aligned} \]

Hence, the relation is also true for \(m+1\).

\[ \begin{equation*} \sum_{v=0}^{n}\binom{v+k}{k}=\binom{n+k+1}{k+1} \tag{A.26} \end{equation*} \]

Proof: We use again an inductive proof on \(n\). The relation is obviously correct for \(n=0\). We assume it is correct for all \(n\) with \(n \leq m\). Then

\[ \begin{aligned} \sum_{\nu=0}^{m+1}\binom{\nu+k}{k} & =\sum_{\nu=0}^{m}\binom{\nu+k}{k}+\binom{\nu+m+1}{m+1} \\ & =\binom{m+k+1}{k+1}+\binom{m+1+k}{k} \end{aligned} \]

Based on equation (A.20), we finally find

\[ \sum_{\nu=0}^{m+1}\binom{\nu+k}{k}=\binom{m+1+k+1}{k+1} \]

and therefore the relation holds for \(m+1\) as well. \(\square\)

Relation (A.26) is equivalent to

\[ \begin{equation*} \sum_{v=0}^{n}\binom{v}{k}=\binom{n+1}{k+1} \tag{A.27} \end{equation*} \]

Proof: According to equation (A.19), the binomial coefficients \(\binom{v}{n}\) are zero for \(v< n\). Hence the sum on the l.h.s. vanishes for \(n<k\), as does the r.h.s. For \(n=k\), the only remaining term on the l.h.s. is \(\binom{n=k}{k}=1\), which again agrees with the r.h.s., \(\binom{k+1}{k+1}=1\). Finally, we consider the case \(n>k\) :

\[ \sum_{\nu=0}^{n}\binom{\nu}{k}=\sum_{\nu=k}^{n}\binom{\nu}{k}=\sum_{\mu=0}^{n-k}\binom{k+\mu}{k} \stackrel{\text { (A.26) }}{=}\binom{k+(n-k)+1}{k+1}, \]

which finishes the proof. \(\square\)

Another relation of binomial coefficients that we want to discuss is

\[ \begin{equation*} S_{n}:=-\sum_{\nu=1}^{n}\binom{n}{\nu} \frac{(-1)^{\nu}}{\nu}=\sum_{\nu=1}^{n} \frac{1}{\nu} . \tag{A.28} \end{equation*} \]

Proof: The value for \(n=1\) is simply \(S_{1}=1\) in both expressions. In order to determine the other values, we consider the differences

\[ \begin{aligned} S_{n+1}-S_{n} & =-\sum_{v=1}^{n+1}\binom{n+1}{v} \frac{(-1)^{v}}{v}+\sum_{v=1}^{n}\binom{n}{v} \frac{(-1)^{v}}{v} \\ & =-\binom{n+1}{n+1} \frac{(-1)^{n+1}}{n+1}-\sum_{v=1}^{n}\left[\binom{n+1}{v}-\binom{n}{v}\right] \frac{(-1)^{v}}{v} \\ & \stackrel{(\text { A. } 20)}{=} \frac{(-1)^{n}}{n+1}-\sum_{v=1}^{n}\binom{n}{v-1} \frac{(-1)^{v}}{v} \end{aligned} \]

\[ \begin{aligned} & =\frac{(-1)^{n}}{n+1}-\sum_{\nu=1}^{n} \frac{n!}{(n-\nu+1)!\nu!}(-1)^{\nu} \\ & =\frac{(-1)^{n}}{n+1}-\frac{1}{n+1} \sum_{v=1}^{n}\binom{n+1}{v}(-1)^{v} \\ & =\frac{(-1)^{n}}{n+1}-\frac{1}{n+1}\left[\sum_{\nu=0}^{n+1}\binom{n+1}{\nu}(-1)^{\nu}\right. \\ & \left.\cdots-\binom{n+1}{n+1}(-1)^{n+1}-\binom{n+1}{0}(-1)^{0}\right] \\ & =\frac{(-1)^{n}}{n+1}-\frac{1}{n+1}\left((1-1)^{n+1}-(-1)^{n+1}-1\right) \\ & =\frac{1}{n+1} \text {. } \end{aligned} \]

This completes the proof, as the differences of \(\sum_{\nu=1}^{n} \frac{1}{\nu}\) yield the same result and both have the same initial value for \(n=1\).

The last relations involving binomial coefficients that shall be discussed are related to the hypergeometric distribution, given by

\[ \begin{equation*} P\left(k_{I} \mid k, n_{I}, n_{I I}\right)=\frac{\binom{n_{I}}{k_{I}}\binom{n_{I I}}{k_{I I}}}{\binom{n}{k}}, \tag{A.29} \end{equation*} \]

with \(k=k_{I}+k_{I I}\) and \(n=n_{I}+n_{I I}\). All quantities are integers in this context. The normalization reads

\[ \sum_{k_{I}=0}^{k}\binom{n_{I}}{k_{I}}\binom{n-n_{I}}{k-k_{I}}=\binom{n}{k} . \]

We will generalize this relation to real numbers \(a\) and \(b\) :

\[ \begin{equation*} \sum_{\nu=0}^{n}\binom{a}{\nu}\binom{b}{n-\nu}=\binom{a+b}{n} \tag{A.30} \end{equation*} \]

Proof: To begin with we note that the sum can be extended all the way up to \(\infty\), since by definition the second binomial is zero for \(v>n\). The sum is then a convolution and the generating function is therefore the product of the generating functions of the individual factors, which are given by

\[ \phi_{a}(z):=\sum_{n=0}^{\infty}\binom{a}{n} x^{n}=(1+z)^{a} . \]

Hence, the generating function of the convolution is

\[ \phi(z)=(1+z)^{a}(1+z)^{b}=(1+z)^{a+b}, \]

which agrees with the generating function of \(\binom{a+b}{n}\). \(\square\)

A special case is \(a=b=n\), for which we find

\[ \begin{equation*} \sum_{n=0}^{n}\binom{n}{v}^{2}=\binom{2 n}{n} . \tag{A.31} \end{equation*} \]

Next we want to show that the mean of the hypergeometric distribution (equation (A.29)) is given by

\[ \begin{equation*} \left\langle k_{I}\right\rangle=n_{I} \frac{k}{n} . \tag{A.32} \end{equation*} \]

Proof: Based on

\[ \begin{equation*} \binom{n}{k}=\frac{n}{k}\binom{n-1}{k-1}, \tag{A.33} \end{equation*} \]

which holds for \(n \geq 1\) and \(k \geq 1\), we find

\[ \binom{n}{k}\left\langle k_{I}\right\rangle=\sum_{k_{I}=0}^{k} k_{I}\binom{n_{I}}{k_{I}}\binom{n_{I I}}{k-k_{I}}=n_{I} \sum_{k_{I}=1}^{k}\binom{n_{I}-1}{k_{I}-1}\binom{n_{I I}}{k-k_{I}} . \]

Next we shift the summation index \(k_{I} \rightarrow k_{I}+1\) :

\[ =n_{I} \sum_{k_{I}=0}^{k-1}\binom{n_{I}-1}{k_{I}}\binom{n_{I I}}{(k-1)-k_{I}} \stackrel{\text { (A.30) }}{=} n_{I}\binom{n-1}{k-1} . \]

So finally we obtain, with equation (A.33),

\[ \left\langle k_{I}\right\rangle=n_{I} \frac{k}{n} . \]

Next comes the proof that the variance of the hypergeometric distribution (equation (A.29)) is given by

\[ \begin{equation*} \operatorname{var}\left(k_{I}\right)=\frac{n_{I} k(n-k) n_{I I}}{n^{2}(n-1)} . \tag{A.34} \end{equation*} \]

Proof: The first step is identical to that in the derivation of the mean:

\[ \begin{aligned} \binom{n}{k}\left\langle k_{I}^{2}\right\rangle & =\sum_{k_{I}=0}^{k} k_{I}^{2}\binom{n_{I}}{k_{I}}\binom{n_{I I}}{k-k_{I}} \\ & =n_{I} \sum_{k_{I}=0}^{k-1}\left(k_{I}+1\right)\binom{n_{I}-1}{k_{I}}\binom{n_{I I}}{k-1-k_{I}} . \end{aligned} \]

Along with equation (A.30), we get

\[ =n_{I}\binom{n-1}{k-1}+n_{I} \sum_{k_{I}=0}^{k-1} k_{I}\binom{n_{I}-1}{k_{I}}\binom{n_{I I}}{k-1-k_{I}} . \]

Repeating these steps yields

\[ \binom{n}{k}\left\langle k_{I}^{2}\right\rangle=n_{I}\binom{n-1}{k-1}+n_{I}\left(n_{I}-1\right)\binom{n-2}{k-2}, \]

and finally

\[ \left\langle k_{I}^{2}\right\rangle=n_{I} \frac{k}{n}+n_{I}\left(n_{I}-1\right) \frac{k(k-1)}{n(n-1)} . \]

Then the variance of the hypergeometric distribution follows from

\[ \begin{aligned} \operatorname{var}\left(k_{I}\right) & =\left\langle k_{I}^{2}\right\rangle-\left\langle k_{I}\right\rangle^{2} \\ & =\frac{n_{I} k}{n}+\frac{n_{I} k(k-1)\left(n_{I}-1\right)}{n(n-1)}-\frac{n_{I}^{2} k^{2}}{n^{2}} \\ & =\frac{n_{I} k(n-k) n_{I I}}{n^{2}(n-1)} . \end{aligned} \]

Appendix B

Selected proofs and derivations

B. 1 Proof of the Neyman-Pearson lemma

Here we want to give a rigorous proof of the Neyman-Pearson (NP) lemma for a simple null tested against a simple alternative. Let the data sample be \(X\). For a predefined significance level \(\alpha\) (probability for type I error),

\[ \begin{equation*} P\left(X \in R \mid H_{0}\right)=\alpha, \tag{B.1} \end{equation*} \]

an optimal rejection region \(R\) is obtained by minimizing the probability for type II error, or equivalently by maximizing the power

\[ \begin{equation*} \max _{A} P\left(X \in A \mid H_{1}\right) . \tag{B.2} \end{equation*} \]

The NP lemma claims that the optimal rejection region of size \(\alpha\) is given by

\[ \begin{equation*} \mathcal{R}=\left\{D \left\lvert\, \frac{p\left(D \mid H_{a}, \mathcal{I}\right)}{p\left(D \mid H_{0}, \mathcal{I}\right)} \geq K_{\alpha}\right.\right\}, \tag{B.3} \end{equation*} \]

where \(K_{\alpha}\) is a constant that has to be adjusted such that equation (B.1) is fulfilled. For the proof we introduce the abbreviations \(P_{\alpha}(A):=P\left(X \in A \mid H_{\alpha}\right)\) for \(\alpha \in\{0, a\}\). Equation (B.3) implies

\[ \begin{array}{ll} P_{a}(X) \geq K_{\alpha} P_{0}(X) & \forall X \in \mathcal{R}, \\ P_{a}(X) \leq K_{\alpha} P_{0}(X) & \forall X \in \overline{\mathcal{R}} . \tag{B.4b} \end{array} \]

First we consider \(X \in \bar{A} \cap \mathcal{R} \subseteq \mathcal{R}\). According to equation (B.4a), we have

\[ P_{a}(\bar{A} \cap \mathcal{R}) \geq K_{\alpha} P_{0}(\bar{A} \cap \mathcal{R}) . \]

Next we consider \(X \in A \cap \overline{\mathcal{R}} \subseteq \overline{\mathcal{R}}\), for which equation (B.4b) holds, resulting in

\[ P_{a}(A \cap \overline{\mathcal{R}}) \leq K_{\alpha} P_{0}(A \cap \overline{\mathcal{R}}) . \]

Let \(\mathcal{R}\) be the optimal rejection region, defined by equation (B.3). For any subset \(A\) of the data space of size \(\alpha\), based on the elementary rules of probability theory we perform the following transformations:

\[ P_{a}(A)=P_{a}(A \cup \mathcal{R})+P_{a}(A \cup \overline{\mathcal{R}})=P_{a}(\mathcal{R})-P_{a}(\bar{A} \cup \mathcal{R})+P_{a}(A \cup \overline{\mathcal{R}}) . \]

Invoking equations (B.4a) and (B.4b), we get

\[ \begin{aligned} P_{a}(A) & \leq P_{a}(\mathcal{R})-K_{\alpha}\left[P_{0}(\bar{A} \cup \mathcal{R})-P_{0}(A \cup \overline{\mathcal{R}})\right] \\ & \leq P_{a}(\mathcal{R})-K_{\alpha}[P_{0}(\mathcal{R}) \underbrace{-P_{0}(A \cup \mathcal{R})-P_{0}(A \cup \overline{\mathcal{R}})}_{=-P_{0}(A)}] . \end{aligned} \]

Finally we use the fact that both \(\mathcal{R}\) and \(A\) have the same size. Therefore, the expression in the bracket vanishes, which finishes the proof:

\[ P_{a}(A) \leq P_{a}(\mathcal{R}) \quad \forall A \text { of size } \alpha . \]

B. 2 Proof of chi-squared for distributions

Here we outline the proof that the sampling distribution for the random variable

\[ x=\sum_{i=1}^{L} \frac{\left(n_{i}-\left\langle n_{i}\right\rangle\right)^{2}}{\left\langle n_{i}\right\rangle}, \quad\left\langle n_{i}\right\rangle=N P_{i} \]

approaches the chi-squared distribution for large \(N\). In order to make the points very clear we consider a specific example, namely the distribution of \(N\) balls in \(L\) boxes. Let the probability for a ball to end in box \(i\) be \(P_{i}\). We introduce a random variable for a single throw of the ball \(b_{i} \in\{0,1\}\), which is 1 if the ball lands in box \(i\) and 0 otherwise. Now we repeat the experiment \(N\) times and obtain for the occupation number of box \(i\)

\[ n_{i}=\sum_{\nu=1}^{N} b_{i}^{(\nu)}, \]

where \(b_{i}^{(\nu)}\) is the random variable for box \(i\) in the \(\nu\) th throw. There is a simple relation between the covariances:

\[ c_{i j}^{(n)}:=\left\langle\Delta n_{i} \Delta n_{j}\right\rangle=\sum_{\nu}\left\langle\Delta b_{i}^{(\nu)} \Delta b_{j}^{(\nu)}\right\rangle+\sum_{\nu \neq \nu^{\prime}}\left\langle\Delta b_{i}^{(\nu)} \Delta b_{j}^{\left(\nu^{\prime}\right)}\right\rangle . \]

Since the variables for different throws are uncorrelated, we find

\[ \begin{gather*} C_{i j}^{(n)}=\underbrace{\sum_{\nu=1}^{N}}_{N} \underbrace{\left\langle\Delta b_{i}^{(\nu)} \Delta b_{j}^{(\nu)}\right\rangle}_{:=C_{i j}^{(b)}}+\sum_{\nu \neq \nu^{\prime}} \underbrace{\left\langle\Delta b_{i}^{(\nu)}\right\rangle}_{=0} \underbrace{\left\langle\Delta b_{j}^{\left(\nu^{\prime}\right)}\right\rangle}_{=0}, \\ C^{(b)}=\frac{1}{N} C^{(n) \stackrel{(4.17 \mathrm{~d})}{=}} \delta_{i j} P_{i}-P_{i} P_{j} . \tag{B.5} \end{gather*} \]

Now it is expedient to introduce the relative occupation of box \(i\), i.e.

\[ \frac{n_{i}}{N}=\frac{1}{N} \sum_{\nu=1}^{N} b_{i}^{(\nu)}=\overline{b_{i}} . \]

The occupation numbers \(n_{i}\) are related to the sample means \(b_{i}\), which is the point where the central limit theorem comes into play. In terms of \(\overline{b_{i}}\), the test statistic reads

\[ \begin{aligned} x & =N \sum_{i=1}^{L} \frac{\left(\overline{b_{i}}-P_{i}\right)^{2}}{P_{i}}=N \sum_{i=1}^{L} y_{i}^{2} \\ y_{i} & :=\left(\overline{b_{i}}-P_{i}\right) P_{i}^{-1 / 2} \end{aligned} \]

Now we switch to vector notation \(y=\left(y_{1}, \ldots, y_{L}\right)^{T}\), and the test statistic simply reads

\[ \begin{equation*} x=N y^{T} y . \tag{B.6} \end{equation*} \]

If it can be shown that the random variables \(y_{i}\) are asymptotically i.i.n.d., then the sampling distribution of \(x\) is chi-squared. Let us check this assumption. The random variable \(y_{i}\) is actually the sample mean of \(\left\{y_{i}^{(\nu)}\right\}\), where the upper index enumerates the members of the sample:

\[ \begin{aligned} y_{i} & =\frac{1}{N} \sum_{\nu=1}^{N} y_{i}^{(\nu)} \\ y_{i}^{(\nu)} & :=\left(b_{i}^{(\nu)}-P_{i}\right) P_{i}^{-1 / 2} \end{aligned} \]

The mean of these random variables \(y_{i}^{(\nu)}\) is zero:

\[ \left\langle y_{i}^{(\nu)}\right\rangle=(\underbrace{\left\langle b_{i}^{(\nu)}\right\rangle}_{P_{i}}-P_{i}) P_{i}^{-1 / 2}=0, \]

and their covariance vanishes if the upper indices are different. The covariance matrix for diagonal upper indices reads

\[ C_{i j}^{(y)}:=\left\langle y_{i}^{(\nu)} y_{j}^{(\nu)}\right\rangle=P_{i}^{-1 / 2} \underbrace{\left\langle\left(b_{i}^{(\nu)}-P_{i}\right)\left(b_{j}^{(\nu)}-P_{j}\right)\right\rangle}_{:=C_{i j}^{(b)}} P_{j}^{-1 / 2} . \]

The covariance matrix is independent of the upper index and has been given in equation (B.5). We obtain

\[ \begin{align*} C_{i j}^{(y)} & =P_{i}^{-1 / 2}\left(P_{i} \delta_{i j}-P_{i} P_{j}\right) P_{j}^{-1 / 2}=\delta_{i j}-\sqrt{P_{i} P_{j}}, \\ \mathbf{C}^{(y)} & =\mathbf{1}-\sqrt{\boldsymbol{P}} \sqrt{\boldsymbol{P}}^{T} \tag{B.7} \end{align*} \]

where \(\sqrt{\boldsymbol{P}}^{\boldsymbol{T}}\) is the row vector \(\left(\sqrt{P_{1}}, \ldots, \sqrt{P_{L}}\right)\). The covariance matrix \(\mathbf{C}^{(y)}\) is real symmetric and idempotent \(\left[\mathbf{C}^{(y)}\right]^{2}=\mathbf{C}^{(y)}\), as one can easily see. So it only has eigenvalues \(\lambda_{l}=\{0,1\}\). Moreover, the trace is

\[ \operatorname{tr}\left(\mathbf{C}^{(y)}\right)=\underbrace{\operatorname{tr}(\mathbf{1})}_{=L}-\underbrace{\sum_{i=1}^{L} P_{i}}_{=1}=L-1 \stackrel{!}{=} \sum_{l=1}^{L} \lambda_{l} \]

and it follows that the eigenvalue \(\lambda=1\) occurs \(L-1\) times and the value \(\lambda=0\) occurs only once. We enumerate the eigenvalues and eigenvectors such that the last eigenvalue is zero, i.e. \(\lambda_{L}=0\). Now we introduce the \(\mathbf{U}\) that contains column-wise the eigenvectors of \(\mathbf{C}^{(y)}\). Hence

\[ \mathbf{U}^{T} \mathbf{C}^{(y)} \mathbf{U}=\operatorname{diag}(\{1, \ldots, 1,0\}) . \]

Since the covariance matrix is real symmetric, \(\mathbf{U}\) is orthogonal: \(\mathbf{U}^{T} \mathbf{U}=\mathbf{1}\). Finally, we introduce new random variables \(z_{l}^{(\nu)}\) by the following orthogonal transformation:

\[ \begin{equation*} z_{l}^{(\nu)}=\sqrt{N} \sum_{i} U_{l i}^{T} y_{i}^{(\nu)} . \tag{B.8} \end{equation*} \]

These variables also have zero mean \(\left\langle z_{l}^{(\nu)}\right\rangle=0\), and the covariance matrix is

\[ \begin{aligned} \left\langle z_{l}^{(\nu)} z_{l^{\prime}}^{(\nu)}\right\rangle & =N \sum_{i j} U_{l i}^{T}\left\langle y_{i}^{(\nu)} y_{j}^{(\nu)}\right\rangle U_{j l}=N\left(\mathbf{U}^{T} \mathbf{C}^{(y)} \mathbf{U}\right)_{l l^{\prime}} \\ & =\operatorname{diag}(\{N, \ldots, N, 0\}) \end{aligned} \]

Since the mean and variance of \(z_{L}^{(\nu)}\) are zero, this quantity is no longer a random variable, instead it is identical to zero. All other random variables \(z_{l}^{(\nu)}\) are now i.i.d., with zero mean and \(\sigma^{2}=N\). So the sample mean

\[ \begin{equation*} z_{l}:=\frac{1}{N} \sum_{\nu} z_{l}^{(\nu)} \tag{B.9} \end{equation*} \]

fulfils all requirements for the central limit theorem and converges for large \(N\) towards the centred unit variance normal \(\mathcal{N}(0,1)\).

Finally, we merely need to express the chi-squared statistic in terms of \(z_{l}\). Owing to equations (B.8) and (B.9), we get

\[ \begin{aligned} z_{l} & =\sqrt{N} \sum_{i=1}^{L} U_{l i}^{T} y_{i}, \\ \sum_{l=1}^{L} z_{l}^{2} & =N \sum_{i, j} \underbrace{\sum_{l} U_{i l} U_{l j}^{T}}_{=\delta_{i j}} y_{i} y_{j}=N \sum_{i} y_{i}^{2}=x . \end{aligned} \]

If we take into account that \(z_{L}\) is zero, then the result is

\[ x=\sum_{l=1}^{L-1} z_{l}^{2} . \]

Hence we are summing up the squares of \(L-1\) central unit variance normal random variables. This proves that the sampling distribution of \(x\) converges for large \(L\) towards the chi-squared distribution with \(L-1\) degrees of freedom.

B. 3 Derivation of Student’s \(\boldsymbol{t}\)-distribution for the difference of means

The topic of this section is the derivation of the sampling distribution of the \(t\)-statistic for difference in means, as defined in equation (19.32) [p. 301]. We can streamline the proof since it contains the same elements as the proof in Section 19.4.1 [p. 299]. Basically the steps are introduction of missing parameters \(\left(\bar{x}_{\alpha}, v_{\alpha}, \sigma, \mu_{0}\right)\), substitution \(s_{\alpha}:=\left(\bar{x}_{\alpha}-\right. \left.\mu_{0}\right) / \sigma, w_{\alpha}:=v_{\alpha} / \sigma^{2}\), elimination of the nuisance parameters \(\sigma\) and \(\mu_{0}\) and evaluation of the remaining integral. For the sake of a lean notation we utilize \(\bar{x}\) for the set \(\left\{\bar{x}_{1}, \bar{x}_{2}\right\}\) and likewise for the other sample-dependent quantities \(N, \boldsymbol{v}, \boldsymbol{s}, \boldsymbol{w}\). In addition, we will use

\[ \mathrm{SE}^{*}:=\sqrt{\frac{N}{(N-2) N_{1} N_{2}}\left(w_{1}+w_{2}\right)} . \]

The proof proceeds as follows:

\[ \begin{aligned} & p(t \mid \boldsymbol{N}, \mathcal{I})= \int \cdots \int d \mu_{0} d \sigma d \overline{\boldsymbol{x}} d \boldsymbol{v} p\left(t \mid \overline{\boldsymbol{x}}, \boldsymbol{v}, \mu_{0}, \sigma, \boldsymbol{N}, \mathcal{I}\right) \cdots \\ & \cdots p\left(\overline{\boldsymbol{x}} \mid \boldsymbol{\phi}, \mu_{0}, \sigma, \boldsymbol{N}, \mathcal{I}\right) p\left(\boldsymbol{v} \mid \mu_{\sigma}, \sigma, \boldsymbol{N}, \mathcal{I}\right) p\left(\mu_{0}, \sigma \mid \boldsymbol{N}, \mathcal{I}\right) \\ &= \ldots \int d \boldsymbol{s} d \boldsymbol{w} \delta\left(t-\frac{s_{2}-s_{1}}{\mathrm{SE}^{*}}\right) \prod_{\alpha} \mathcal{N}\left(s_{\alpha} \mid 0, \frac{1}{\sqrt{N_{\alpha}}}\right) \cdots \\ & \prod_{\alpha} p \Gamma\left(w_{\alpha} \left\lvert\, \frac{N_{\alpha}-1}{2}\right., \frac{1}{2}\right) \underbrace{\iint d \mu_{0} d \sigma p\left(\mu_{0}, \sigma \mid \boldsymbol{N}, \mathcal{I}\right)}_{=1} \\ & \propto \int d \boldsymbol{w} \underbrace{\int d s_{1} \mathcal{N}\left(s_{1} \mid 0, \frac{1}{\sqrt{N_{1}}}\right) \mathcal{N}\left(s_{1}+t \mathrm{SE}^{*} \mid 0, \frac{1}{\sqrt{N_{2}}}\right)}_{\propto \exp \left\{-\frac{t^{2}\left(w_{1}+w_{2}\right)}{2(N-2)}\right\}} \cdots \\ & \ldots \sqrt{w_{1}+w_{2}} \prod_{\alpha} w_{\alpha}^{\frac{N_{\alpha}-3}{2}} e^{-\frac{w_{\alpha}}{2}} \\ & \propto \int \frac{d w_{1}}{w_{1}} \frac{d w_{2}}{w_{2}} \sqrt{w_{1}+w_{2}} \prod_{\alpha} w_{\alpha}^{\frac{N_{\alpha}-1}{2}} e^{-\frac{w_{\alpha}}{2}\left(1+\frac{t^{2}}{N-2}\right)} \end{aligned} \]

Finally, the substitution \(w_{\alpha}\left(1+\frac{t^{2}}{N-2}\right)=x_{\alpha}\) yields

\[ \begin{aligned} p(t \mid N, \mathcal{I}) & \propto\left(1+\frac{t^{2}}{N-2}\right)^{-\frac{N-2+1}{2}} \underbrace{\int \frac{d x_{1}}{x_{1}} \frac{d x_{2}}{x_{2}} \sqrt{x_{1}+x_{2}} \prod_{\alpha} x_{\alpha}^{\frac{N_{\alpha}-1}{2}} e^{-\frac{x_{\alpha}}{2}}}_{=\text {const. }} \\ & \propto\left(1+\frac{t^{2}}{v}\right)^{-\frac{v+1}{2}} \end{aligned} \]

This finishes the proof that \(t\) is distributed as \(p_{\Gamma}(t \mid v)\), with \(v=N-2\).

B. 4 Variance of MCMC sample mean

Here we outline the computation of the variance of the sample mean depicted in equation (30.50) [p. 562]:

\[ \begin{aligned} a(\Delta \tau) & =\left\langle\Delta f\left(\boldsymbol{x}_{\Delta \tau}\right) \Delta f\left(\boldsymbol{x}_{0}\right)\right\rangle=\left\langle f\left(\boldsymbol{x}_{\Delta \tau}\right) f\left(\boldsymbol{x}_{0}\right)\right\rangle-\langle f\rangle^{2} \\ & =\sum_{i, j} f_{i} f_{j} P\left(\boldsymbol{x}_{\Delta \tau}=\boldsymbol{\xi}_{j}, \boldsymbol{x}_{0}=\boldsymbol{\xi}_{i} \mid p^{0}, \mathcal{I}\right)-\langle f\rangle^{2} \\ & =\sum_{i, j} f_{i} f_{j} P\left(\boldsymbol{x}_{\Delta \tau}=\boldsymbol{\xi}_{j} \mid \boldsymbol{x}_{0}=\boldsymbol{\xi}_{i}, \mathcal{I}\right) \underbrace{P\left(\boldsymbol{x}_{0}=\boldsymbol{\xi}_{i} \mid p^{0}, \mathcal{I}\right)}_{\rho_{i}}-\langle f\rangle^{2} \\ & =\sum_{i, j} f_{j}\left(\left(M^{\prime \Delta \tau}\right)_{j i}+\rho_{j}\right) \rho_{i} f_{i}-\langle f\rangle^{2} \\ & =\sum_{i, j}\left(\rho_{j} f_{j}\right)\left(\boldsymbol{M}^{\prime \Delta \tau}\right)_{j i}\left(\rho_{i} f_{i}\right)+\langle f\rangle^{2}-\langle f\rangle^{2} \\ & =\sum_{l=2}^{\mathcal{N}} \underbrace{\left(\sum_{j} f_{j} x_{j l}\right)}_{=\tilde{f}_{l}} \lambda_{l}^{\Delta \tau} \underbrace{\left(\sum_{i} \rho_{i} f_{i} \frac{X_{i l}^{*}}{\rho_{i}}\right)}_{=\tilde{f}_{l}^{*}} \\ & =\sum_{l=2}^{\mathcal{N}}\left|\tilde{f}_{l}\right|^{2} \lambda_{l}^{\Delta \tau} . \end{aligned} \]

Hence the normalized autocorrelation reads

NORMALIZED AUTOCORRELATION

\[ \tilde{a}(\Delta \tau)=\sum_{l=2}^{\mathcal{N}} \frac{\left|\tilde{f}_{l}\right|^{2}}{\sigma_{f}^{2}} \lambda_{l}^{\Delta \tau} . \]

Then we find for the variance of the sample mean

\[ \left\langle(\Delta S)^{2}\right\rangle=\frac{\sigma_{f}^{2}}{N}(1+\sum_{l=2}^{\mathcal{N}} \frac{\left|\tilde{f}_{l}\right|^{2}}{\sigma_{f}^{2}} \underbrace{\frac{2}{N} \sum_{\Delta \tau=1}^{N-1}(N-\Delta \tau) \lambda_{l}^{\Delta \tau}}_{:=\kappa\left(\lambda_{l}\right)}) . \]

Now, we merely need to compute \(\kappa(\lambda)\) :

\[ \frac{N}{2} h(\lambda)=N \sum_{\mu=1}^{N-1} \lambda^{\mu}-\sum_{\mu=1}^{N-1} \mu \lambda^{\mu}=N \lambda \frac{1-\lambda^{N-1}}{1-\lambda}-\sum_{\mu=1}^{N-1} \mu \lambda^{\mu}, \]

\[ \begin{aligned} \sum_{\mu=1}^{N-1} \mu \lambda^{\mu} & =\lambda \frac{\partial}{\partial \lambda} \sum_{\mu=1}^{N-1} \lambda^{\mu}=-\frac{N \lambda}{1-\lambda}\left(\lambda^{N-1}-\frac{1-\lambda^{N}}{N(1-\lambda)}\right) \\ \kappa(\lambda) & =\frac{2 \lambda}{(1-\lambda)}\left(1-\frac{1}{N} \frac{1-\lambda^{N}}{1-\lambda}\right) . \end{aligned} \]

Collecting all terms, we have the desired result:

Variance of sample mean for equilibrated Markov chains

\[ \begin{aligned} \left\langle(\Delta S)^{2}\right\rangle & =\sigma_{f}^{2}\left[\frac{1}{N}+\sum_{l=2}^{\mathcal{N}} \frac{\left|\tilde{f}_{l}\right|^{2}}{\sigma_{f}^{2}} \kappa_{l}\right] \\ \kappa_{l} & :=\frac{2 \lambda_{l}}{\left(1-\lambda_{l}\right)}\left(1-\frac{1}{N} \frac{1-\lambda_{l}^{N}}{1-\lambda_{l}}\right) \end{aligned} \]

B. 5 Covariance for nested sampling

Here we outline the derivation of the covariance required for nested sampling. The covariance matrix \(\left\langle\Delta X_{n^{\prime}}^{*} \Delta X_{n}^{*}\right\rangle\) is symmetric and we can restrict the discussion to \(n^{\prime}=n+m\), with \(m \geq 0\). In the following derivation we use equation (31.14) [p. 580]:

\[ \begin{aligned} \left\langle X_{n+m}^{*} X_{n}^{*}\right\rangle & =\left\langle\prod_{\nu=1}^{n+m} \theta_{\nu} \prod_{\nu^{\prime}=1}^{n} \theta_{\nu^{\prime}}\right\rangle \\ & =\prod_{\nu=n+1}^{n+m}\left\langle\theta_{\nu}\right\rangle \prod_{\nu^{\prime}=1}^{n}\left\langle\theta_{\nu^{\prime}}^{2}\right\rangle \\ & =\left(\frac{K}{K+1}\right)^{m}\left(\frac{K}{K+2}\right)^{n} \\ \left\langle\Delta X_{n+m}^{*} \Delta X_{n}^{*}\right\rangle & =\left(\frac{K}{K+1}\right)^{m}\left(\frac{K}{K+2}\right)^{n}-\left(\frac{K}{K+1}\right)^{m+n}\left(\frac{K}{K+1}\right)^{n} \\ & =X_{n+m} X_{n}\left(\left[1+\frac{1}{K(K+2)}\right]^{n}-1\right) \\ & =X_{n+m} X_{n}\left(e^{n \ln (1+1 / K(K+2))}-1\right) . \end{aligned} \]

Now

\[ X_{n}=\left(\frac{K}{K+1}\right)^{n} \quad \Rightarrow \ln \left(X_{n}\right)=-n \ln (1+1 / K), \]

and hence

\[ \begin{aligned} \left\langle\Delta X_{n+m}^{*} \Delta X_{n}^{*}\right\rangle & =X_{n+m} X_{n}\left(e^{-\ln \left(X_{n}\right) \kappa}-1\right) \\ \kappa & :=\frac{\ln (1+1 / K(K+2))}{\ln (1+1 / K)} \\ & =\frac{1}{K}\left(1-\frac{3}{2 K}+O\left(\frac{1}{K^{2}}\right)\right) \end{aligned} \]

So the final result for \(n^{\prime} \geq n\) reads

\[ \left\langle\Delta X_{n^{\prime}}^{*} \Delta X_{n}^{*}\right\rangle=X_{n^{\prime}} X_{n}\left(X_{n}^{-\kappa}-1\right) . \]

From the basics to the forefront of modern research, this book presents all aspects of probability theory, statistics and data analysis from a Bayesian perspective for physicists and engineers.

The book presents the roots, applications and numerical implementation of probability theory, and covers advanced topics such as maximum entropy distributions, stochastic processes, parameter estimation, model selection, hypothesis testing and experimental design. In addition, it explores state-of-theart numerical techniques required to solve demanding real-world problems. The book is ideal for students and researchers in physical sciences and engineering.

Wolfgang von der Linden is Professor of Theoretical and Computational Physics at the Graz University of Technology. His research area is statistical physics, with a focus on strongly correlated quantum many-body physics based on computational techniques.

Volker Dose is a former Director of the Surface Physics Division of the Max Planck Institute for Plasma Physics. He has contributed to Bayesian methods in physics, astronomy and climate research.

Udo von Toussaint is a Senior Scientist in the Material Research Division of the Max Planck Institute for Plasma Physics, where he works on Bayesian experimental design, data fusion, molecular dynamics and inverse problems in the field of plasma-wall interactions.

## Nested sampling

### 31.1 Motivation

An interesting and promising complementary approach for the stochastic estimation of high-dimensional integrals of the form

$$
Z:=\int d^{N} x L(x) p(x)
$$

which may be evidence integrals or partition functions, has been proposed by John Skilling [65, 103, 188, 191-193]. It is widely applicable and allows us also to treat problems where the likelihood is extremely peaked in tiny regions in $\boldsymbol{x}$-space on an otherwise almost flat background. In such cases all MCMC techniques discussed so far are inefficient. Lebesgue once compared the brute-force evaluation of integrals via Riemann summation over $\boldsymbol{x}$ with an unsystematic merchant, who counts his money in the random order he receives it. A prudent merchant however, he claimed, would count his money by first grouping the coins and banknotes according to their respective values and counting how often the individual values occur [61]. This is precisely what Skilling suggested [188, 191]. He expresses the integral as

$$
\begin{align*}
Z & =\int d \lambda X(\lambda)  \tag{31.1a}\\
X(\lambda) & :=\int d^{N} x p(x) \mathbb{I}(L(x)>\lambda) \tag{31.1b}
\end{align*}
$$

where $X(\lambda)$ is the prior mass of regions where the likelihood exceeds a threshold $\lambda$. One verifies equation (31.1a) immediately:

$$
\int d \lambda X(\lambda)=\int d^{N} x p(\boldsymbol{x}) \underbrace{\int d \lambda \mathbb{I}(\lambda<L(\boldsymbol{x}))}_{=L(\boldsymbol{x})}=Z .
$$

For a general discussion on the mapping of high-dimensional integrals to one-dimensional integrals, see Section 7.3 [p. 96]. Clearly, $X(\lambda)$ decreases monotonically with increasing $\lambda$. For the following considerations we need strict monotonicity, which can always be achieved by adding a suitable infinitesimal function $X(\lambda) \rightarrow X(\lambda)+\eta(\lambda)$ with $\eta(\lambda) \rightarrow 0$ in the final results. Now we can define the inverse function $L(X)$ by $L(X(\lambda))=\lambda$ and vice

```{r}
#| code-fold: true
#| code-summary: "Show plot"
plot(1, main="")
```
Figure 31.1 Sketch of the nested sampling concept. Meaning of columns from left to right: $L(x)$, $p(x)$ and $L(X)$. For four different threshold values of the likelihood $\lambda_{4}=7, \lambda_{3}=5, \lambda_{2}=3$ and $\lambda_{1}=0.5$ the corresponding prior masses $X_{1}, \ldots, X_{4}$ are indicated. For further discussion see text.

versa, $X(L(\xi))=\xi$. Note that we here overload the symbol $L$ for likelihood value, allowing it to be not just a function of position $\boldsymbol{x}$ but also (and equivalently) a function of prior mass $X$, according to the type of its argument. Then integration by parts of equation (31.1a) yields

$$
\begin{equation*}
Z=\int_{0}^{1} d X L(X) \tag{31.2}
\end{equation*}
$$

These ideas can be illustrated by a simple one-dimensional problem with only discrete likelihood values. $L(x)$ is depicted in the left panel of Figure 31.1. The prior PDF $p(x)$ is shown in the middle panel. The rows of the left panel correspond to different threshold values $\lambda_{\nu}$, indicated by dashed horizontal lines. The constraint intervals of $x$ values for which $L(x)>\lambda$ are shown in a lighter shade, both in the likelihood and prior graphs. The integral of the prior over these marked areas yields the prior mass $X(\lambda)$ for the underlying $\lambda$ value. The result is printed in the corresponding subfigure. Finally, the rightmost figure depicts $L(X)$ vs $X$. In this figure we can read off the relation between $\lambda_{\nu}$ and the corresponding $X_{\nu}=X\left(\lambda_{\nu}\right)$, as indicated by the connecting lines. The inverse relation $X(\lambda)$ vs $\lambda$ is obtained by merely swapping the axes.

In order to evaluate the integral numerically, we convert it into a Riemann sum. To this end we introduce a sequence $X_{n}$ of $X$ values

$$
\begin{equation*}
X_{n}:=\zeta^{n}, \quad \zeta:=\frac{K}{K+1}, \tag{31.3}
\end{equation*}
$$

which will be more fully explained shortly. The values $X_{n}$ become dense in the limit $K \rightarrow \infty$ and we express the integral by

Riemann sum

$$
\begin{align*}
Z_{K} & :=\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} L\left(X_{n}\right)\left(X_{n}-X_{n+1}\right),  \tag{31.4a}\\
X_{n}-X_{n+1} & =\zeta^{n} \frac{1}{K+1} . \tag{31.4b}
\end{align*}
$$

Since $L(X)$ decreases monotonically over $X, Z_{K}$ is a lower bound for $Z$. Likewise, an upper bound is obtain by

$$
Z_{K}^{u}:=\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} L\left(X_{n}\right)\left(X_{n-1}-X_{n}\right)=\zeta^{-1} Z_{K} .
$$

If $L$ has a nonzero minimum value $\left(L\left(X_{0}\right)=L(1) \neq 0\right.$ ), this lower limit can be improved by summing from $n=0$. Hence, the quadrature error is well controlled through lower and upper bounds:

## UPPER AND LOWER BOUNDS FOR $Z$

$$
\begin{equation*}
Z_{K} \leq Z \leq\left(1+\frac{1}{K}\right) Z_{K} . \tag{31.5}
\end{equation*}
$$

The quadrature error can easily be improved to order $O\left(1 / K^{2}\right)$ by the trapezoidal rule, but that is unnecessary as the statistical uncertainties will dominate the numerical error. So far, this is very nice but not really useful, since $L\left(X_{n}\right)$ is not at our disposal yet.

### 31.1.1 Nested sampling

Skilling proposed a stochastic approach to sample $L(X)$ based on an order statistic, which he called nested sampling. It is based on the (conditional) prior

$$
\begin{equation*}
p(x \mid \lambda):=\frac{p(x)}{X(\lambda)} \mathbb{1}(L(x)>\lambda), \tag{31.6}
\end{equation*}
$$

which is proportional to the original one, however, restricted to regions in parameter space $\boldsymbol{x}$ for which $L(\boldsymbol{x})$ is above the given threshold $\lambda$. The nested sampling algorithm can be summarized in the following few lines:

## Nested sampling algorithm

- Initiate $\lambda_{0}^{*}=0, n=0$.
- Until convergence, iterate the following lines:

1. Increment the iteration count $n \rightarrow n+1$.
2. Draw a sample $\left\{x_{i}\right\}$ of size $K$ from $p\left(x \mid \lambda_{n-1}^{*}\right)$.
3. Compute the corresponding $L$ values $\lambda_{i}=L\left(\boldsymbol{x}_{i}\right)$.
4. Determine the sample minimum $\lambda_{n}^{*}=\min _{i} \lambda_{i}$.

- Set $n_{\text {max }}=n$.

Then the stochastic estimator is

Skilling estimate of $\boldsymbol{Z}$

$$
\begin{equation*}
\hat{Z}_{K}:=\sum_{n=1}^{n_{\max }} \lambda_{n}^{*}\left(X_{n}^{*}-X_{n+1}^{*}\right) \tag{31.7}
\end{equation*}
$$

Nested sampling only yields the likelihood values $\lambda_{n}^{*}$. These values uniquely define the corresponding prior mass values $X_{n}^{*}$ through the relation

$$
\begin{equation*}
\lambda_{n}^{*}=L\left(X_{n}^{*}\right) \tag{31.8}
\end{equation*}
$$

However, since the functional form of $L(X)$ is not known, we replace $X_{n}^{*}$ by $X_{n}$, defined in equation (31.4b):

$$
\begin{equation*}
\tilde{Z}_{K}:=\sum_{n=1}^{n_{\max }} \lambda_{n}^{*}\left(X_{n}-X_{n+1}\right) \tag{31.9}
\end{equation*}
$$

The reason will become clear later on. The sample size $K$, or rather the number of walkers as we will call them in this context, defines the quantity $\zeta$ that in turn determines the quadrature points in equation (31.4a). Needless to say, $\tilde{Z}_{K}$ is a random variable just like the sample minima $\lambda_{n}^{*}$. It is noteworthy that step 2 of the nested sampling algorithm can be simplified significantly. Instead of generating an entirely new sample, for $n>1$ the elements of the previous sample can be recycled and merely the sample minimum $\lambda_{n-1}^{*}$ of the previous iteration needs to be replaced. The other elements constitute already a valid
random sample drawn from the conditional prior. Since $\lambda_{n}^{*}$ is a monotonically increasing series, we can estimate the residue based on equation (31.7):

$$
R_{n_{\max }}:=\sum_{n=n_{\max }+1}^{\infty} \lambda_{n}^{*}\left(X_{n}-X_{n+1}\right) .
$$

Once the sample minima $\lambda_{n}^{*}$ have converged, i.e. $\lambda_{n}^{*}$ no longer changes over the course of the iterations, we can estimate the residue of the sum:

$$
R_{n_{\max }} \approx \lambda_{n_{\max }}^{*} \sum_{n=n_{\max }+1}^{\infty} \Delta X_{n}=\lambda_{n_{\max }}^{*} X_{n_{\max }} .
$$

Then, the error introduced by stopping the run after a finite number of steps reads

## Truncation error

$$
\begin{equation*}
R_{n_{\max }} \approx \lambda_{n_{\max }}^{*} \zeta^{n_{\max }} \tag{31.10}
\end{equation*}
$$

$R_{n_{\text {max }}}$ is a possible stopping criterion for the nested sampling run, provided $\lambda_{n}^{*}$ levels off in an acceptable number of steps. This can, however, be dangerous if the likelihood consists of narrow peaks on an otherwise smooth background. Then $\lambda_{n}^{*}$ can be rather flat for several iterations until the contributions from the peaks set in. In some cases the maximum of the likelihood $L_{\text {max }}$ is known and can be used to decide on convergence. This would be a safe stopping criterion, but as we will see below, it might be overcautious. The computational cost required for a desired accuracy, which is proportional to $n_{\text {max }}$, depends strongly on the problem. A different convergence criterion will be given in Section 31.3 [p. 591 ].

By now we know the quadrature and truncation error. But we still have to prove that equation (31.7) defines a sensible estimator for $Z_{K}$ and on top of that we need to assess the stochastic uncertainty. Before elaborating on these aspects, we will illustrate the features of nested sampling guided by a simple and comprehensible example.

### 31.1.2 Illustrative example

A suitable model to study the behaviour of nested sampling is given by the 1-D example introduced for the assessment of simulated tempering, given by equation (30.58) [p. 566]. The likelihood possesses two well-separated peaks, that might be associated with two equivalent minima in the free energy, mimicking broken symmetry states but only in one (effective) dimension. High-dimensional problems will be discussed later on. In Figure 31.2 we sketch the $L$-dependence of the prior masses $X_{i}$, represented by the shaded areas above the lines $\lambda_{i}$. Clearly, for $L>L_{\max }\left(L_{\max } \approx 0.8\right.$ in this example), $X$ vanishes. Lowering $L$ below $L_{\text {max }}$ yields a slow increase in $X$ corresponding to the increasing interval size (prior mass) defined by the width of the highest peak. The decrease
